                    }
                }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome biology of the paleotetraploid perennial biomass crop Miscanthus"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2020 11:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-10-28"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Miscanthus is a perennial wild grass that is of global importance for paper production, roofing, horticultural plantings, and an emerging highly productive temperate biomass crop. We report a chromosome-scale assembly of the paleotetraploid M. sinensis genome, providing a resource for Miscanthus that links its chromosomes to the related diploid Sorghum and complex polyploid sugarcanes. The asymmetric distribution of transposons across the two homoeologous subgenomes proves Miscanthus paleo-allotetraploidy and identifies several balanced reciprocal homoeologous exchanges. Analysis of M. sinensis and M. sacchariflorus populations demonstrates extensive interspecific admixture and hybridization, and documents the origin of the highly productive triploid bioenergy crop M. &#215; giganteus. Transcriptional profiling of leaves, stem, and rhizomes over growing seasons provides insight into rhizome development and nutrient recycling, processes critical for sustainable biomass accumulation in a perennial temperate grass. The Miscanthus genome expands the power of comparative genomics to understand traits of importance to Andropogoneae grasses. The perennial grass Miscanthus is a promising biomass crop. Here, via genomics and transcriptomics, the authors reveal its allotetraploid origin, characterize gene expression associated with rhizome development and nutrient recycling, and describe the hybrid origin of the triploid M. x giganteus."/>

Here, via genomics and transcriptomics, the authors reveal its allotetraploid origin, characterize gene expression associated with rhizome development and nutrient recycling, and describe the hybrid origin of the triploid M. x giganteus."/>

            <li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/agricultural-genetics" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">Agricultural genetics</a></li><li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/genetic-variation" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">Genetic variation</a></li><li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/plant-genetics" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">Plant genetics</a></li>
        </ul>

            </div>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Miscanthus</i> is a perennial wild grass that is of global importance for paper production, roofing, horticultural plantings, and an emerging highly productive temperate biomass crop. We report a chromosome-scale assembly of the paleotetraploid <i>M. sinensis</i> genome, providing a resource for <i>Miscanthus</i> that links its chromosomes to the related diploid <i>Sorghum</i> and complex polyploid sugarcanes. The asymmetric distribution of transposons across the two homoeologous subgenomes proves <i>Miscanthus</i> paleo-allotetraploidy and identifies several balanced reciprocal homoeologous exchanges. Analysis of <i>M. sinensis</i> and <i>M. sacchariflorus</i> populations demonstrates extensive interspecific admixture and hybridization, and documents the origin of the highly productive triploid bioenergy crop <i>M. × giganteus</i>. Transcriptional profiling of leaves, stem, and rhizomes over growing seasons provides insight into rhizome development and nutrient recycling, processes critical for sustainable biomass accumulation in a perennial temperate grass. The <i>Miscanthus</i> genome expands the power of comparative genomics to understand traits of importance to Andropogoneae grasses.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>In addition to its historical roles in paper production and as ornamentals, varieties of the wild grass <i>Miscanthus</i> can produce high yields of harvestable vegetative biomass while maintaining and potentially increasing soil carbon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Jones, M. B., Zimmermann, J. &amp; Clifton-Brown, J. Long-Term Yields and Soil Carbon Sequestration from Miscanthus: A Review. In (Barth, S., Murphy-Bokern, D., Kalinina, O., Taylor, G., Jones, M. (eds)) Perennial Biomass Crops for a Resource-Constrained World. Springer, Cham. 43–49 &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-44530-4_4&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (Springer, 2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1429">1</a></sup>. These features, enabled by C4 photosynthesis, perenniality, and related high efficiencies of light, nutrient, and water use, make <i>Miscanthus</i> and its close relatives (including sugarcanes and energy canes) promising candidates for economically feasible and sustainable bioenergy crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Langholtz, M. H., Stokes, B. J. &amp; Eaton, L. M. 2016 Billion-ton report: advancing domestic resources for a thriving bioeconomy, volume 1: economic availability of feedstock, 1–411 (OakRidge National Laboratory, Oak Ridge, Tennessee, UT-Battelle, LLC for the US Department of Energy, 2016)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1436">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Long, S. P. et al. in Bioenergy &amp; Sustainability: Bridging the Gaps, Vol. 72 (eds Souza, G. M., Victoria, R., Joly, C. &amp; Verdade, L.) 302–336 (SCOPE, 2015)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1436_1">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Committee on Climate Change. Net Zero—The UK’s Contribution to Stopping Global Warming. Committee on Climate Change. &#xA;                  https://www.theccc.org.uk/publication/net-zero-the-uks-contribution-to-stopping-global-warming/&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1439">4</a></sup>. Continued genetic improvement of bioenergy feedstocks is needed to enhance productivity and ensure that these crops remain robust in the face of ongoing biotic and abiotic stresses. This is particularly true for perennial grasses, where the advantages in economic and environmental sustainability relative to annuals depend on the longevity of the crop once established. Although perennial crops have tremendous potential for maximizing agricultural yields and minimizing environmental impacts, our knowledge of their biology and ability to manipulate their genetics lags well behind that in annual crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Kantar, M. B. et al. Perennial grain and oilseed crops. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 67, 703–729 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1443">5</a></sup>.</p><p>A key limitation to the genetic improvement of perennial bioenergy grasses is the complexity of their genomes, which hinders the application of modern breeding approaches<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bevan, M. W. et al. Genomic innovation for crop improvement. Nature 543, 346–354 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1450">6</a></sup>. <i>Miscanthus sinensis</i> is a genetic diploid (2<i>n</i> = 38) with a genome size of 1C = 2.4–2.6 Gb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Rayburn, A. L., Crawford, J., Rayburn, C. M. &amp; Juvik, J. A. Genome size of three Miscanthus species. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 27, 184–188 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1460">7</a></sup>; the related <i>M. sacchariflorus</i> occurs in both diploid (2<i>n</i> = 38) and tetraploid (2<i>n</i> = 76) forms. The <i>n</i> = 19 monoploid chromosome set of <i>Miscanthus</i> arose by ancient doubling of a sorghum-like <i>n</i> = 10 ancestor, with a single chromosomal fusion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Swaminathan, K. et al. A framework genetic map for Miscanthus sinensis from RNAseq-based markers shows recent tetraploidy. BMC Genom. 13, 142 (2012)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1483">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ma, X.-F. et al. High resolution genetic mapping by genome sequencing reveals genome duplication and tetraploid genetic structure of the diploid Miscanthus sinensis. PLoS One 7, e33821 (2012)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1483_1">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Kim, C. et al. SSR-based genetic maps of Miscanthus sinensis and M. sacchariflorus, and their comparison to sorghum. Theor. Appl. Genet. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s00122-012-1790-1&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1486">10</a></sup>. Interspecific hybrids of <i>Miscanthus</i> form readily, even between individuals of different ploidy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Clark, L. V. et al. Genetic structure of Miscanthus sinensis and Miscanthus sacchariflorus in Japan indicates a gradient of bidirectional but asymmetric introgression. J. Exp. Bot. 66, 4213–4225 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1494">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Clark, L. V. et al. Population structure of Miscanthus sacchariflorus reveals two major polyploidization events, tetraploid-mediated unidirectional introgression from diploid M. sinensis, and diversity centred around the Yellow Sea. Ann. Bot. &#xA;                  https://academic.oup.com/aob/advance-article-abstract/doi/10.1093/aob/mcy161/5104475&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1497">12</a></sup>. Indeed, the predominant commercially grown miscanthus bioenergy variety is the high-yielding, sterile, asexually propagated triploid hybrid <i>M. × giganteus</i> “Illinois” (3<i>n</i> = 57). It is a clone of the taxonomic-type specimen, holotypus 1993–1780 Kew<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Hodkinson, T. R. &amp; Renvoize, S. Nomenclature of Miscanthus x giganteus (Poaceae). Kew Bull. 56, 759 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1507">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Głowacka, K. et al. Genetic variation in Miscanthus× giganteus and the importance of estimating genetic distance thresholds for differentiating clones. GCB Bioenergy 7, 386–404 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1510">14</a></sup>. Polyploidy is also common within the <i>Saccharum</i> complex, a group of closely related and highly productive perennial C4 grass species in the subtribe Saccharinae that includes sugarcanes (<i>Saccharum</i> spp.) and miscanthus. Intergeneric hybrid “miscanes” have been made by crossing miscanthus with hybrid sugarcanes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Kar, S. et al. Saccharum × Miscanthus intergeneric hybrids (miscanes) exhibit greater chilling tolerance of C 4 photosynthesis and postchilling recovery than sugarcane (Saccharum spp. hybrids). GCB Bioenergy 49, 225 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1521">15</a></sup>, suggesting that natural genetic variation in these two genera could be combined in order to blend desirable traits (e.g., cold tolerance and disease resistance).</p><p>Here we establish miscanthus as a genomic model for perenniality and polyploidy, and develop a foundation for genomic variation that will enable the future improvement of perennial biomass crops. We describe a draft chromosome-scale genome sequence for <i>M. sinensis</i>, prove that miscanthus is a paleo-allotetraploid by analyzing the distribution of transposable elements across its genome, and establish the timing of key evolutionary events. By mRNA sequencing, we identify genes preferentially expressed in rhizomes, stems, and leaves, and explore the unique transcriptional dynamics of nutrient mobilization in this rhizomatous perennial grass. Unlike most perennial Andropogoneae, which are restricted to tropical or subtropical regions, the <i>Miscanthus</i> genus comprises species that naturally range from tropical to subarctic regions. Genomic analysis of 18 miscanthus accessions sequenced for this study, in addition to reduced representation genotyping of over 2000 accessions collected in the wild from east Asia, reveals extensive population structure and interspecific introgression, which further contributes to the genomic diversity of the genus <i>Miscanthus</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequence and organization</h3><p>We assembled the <i>M. sinensis</i> genome into <i>n</i> = 19 chromosomes by combining short-read whole-genome shotgun (WGS) and fosmid-end data with in vitro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Putnam, N. H. et al. Chromosome-scale shotgun assembly using an in vitro method for long-range linkage. Genome Res. 26, 342–350 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1555">16</a></sup> and in vivo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119–1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1559">17</a></sup> chromatin proximity libraries (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">1</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">1</a>, and Supplementary Notes <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">2</a>). The reference accession is the previously characterized<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Swaminathan, K. et al. A framework genetic map for Miscanthus sinensis from RNAseq-based markers shows recent tetraploidy. BMC Genom. 13, 142 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1576">8</a></sup> doubled haploid DH1, which as expected is homozygous throughout. The genome assembly anchors 1.68 Gb of contigs to chromosomes, with a contig N50 length of 33.1 kb and pre-HiC scaffolding N50 length of 190 kb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">2</a>). An additional 0.20 Gb of contig sequence in scaffolds is not yet placed on linkage groups; highly repetitive sequences are problematic and missing from the assembly (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">1b</a>). We validated the assembly at chromosome scale by comparison with an integrated genetic map with 4298 assignable markers (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><p>We predicted the structure of 67,967 protein-coding genes based on several lines of evidence, including homology with other grasses and deep transcriptome data for miscanthus and sugarcane<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Vettore, A. L. et al. Analysis and functional annotation of an expressed sequence tag collection for tropical crop sugarcane. Genome Res. 13, 2725–2735 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1593">18</a></sup>. These predicted genes account for an estimated 98% of protein-coding genes, with 94% assigned to a chromosomal position (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">3</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">5</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">5</a>, and Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">4</a>). These genes are embedded within a sea of transposable element relicts and other repetitive sequences, which account for 72.4% of the <i>M. sinensis</i> genome assembly. The most common class of assembled transposons are gypsy long-terminal-repeat (LTR) retrotransposons (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">6</a> and Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">5</a>).</p><p>The paleotetraploidy of miscanthus is evident at the sequence level, since each sorghum chromosome aligns to a pair of <i>M. sinensis</i> chromosomes, after accounting for the chromosome fusion of ancestral sorghum 4- and 7-like chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Swaminathan, K. et al. A framework genetic map for Miscanthus sinensis from RNAseq-based markers shows recent tetraploidy. BMC Genom. 13, 142 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1625">8</a></sup> that reduces the karyotype from <i>n</i> = 20 to <i>n</i> = 19 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig1">1a</a>). As expected from earlier genetic maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Swaminathan, K. et al. A framework genetic map for Miscanthus sinensis from RNAseq-based markers shows recent tetraploidy. BMC Genom. 13, 142 (2012)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1639">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ma, X.-F. et al. High resolution genetic mapping by genome sequencing reveals genome duplication and tetraploid genetic structure of the diploid Miscanthus sinensis. PLoS One 7, e33821 (2012)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1639_1">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Kim, C. et al. SSR-based genetic maps of Miscanthus sinensis and M. sacchariflorus, and their comparison to sorghum. Theor. Appl. Genet. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s00122-012-1790-1&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1642">10</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">3</a>), the miscanthus and sorghum genomes show extensive 2:1 conserved collinear synteny (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig1">1a</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">4a</a>), consistent with a whole-genome duplication in the <i>Miscanthus</i> lineage. While it has been suggested<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Kim, C. et al. Comparative analysis of Miscanthus and Saccharum reveals a shared whole-genome duplication but different evolutionary fates. Plant Cell 26, 2420–2429 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1658">19</a></sup> that this duplication could be shared with sugarcane, comparison of <i>M. sinensis</i> and <i>S. spontaneum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Zhang, J. et al. Allele-defined genome of the autopolyploid sugarcane Saccharum spontaneum L. Nat. Genet. 50, 1565–1573 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1668">20</a></sup> genomes shows that the duplications in the two lineages are distinct (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">7</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig2">2</a>). Although the doubled genome and disomic genetics of miscanthus is suggestive of an allotetraploid history, neither a mechanism nor timing for paleotetraploidy has been described, in part due to the absence of known diploid progenitor lineages. We address this further below.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Allotetraploidy in miscanthus."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Allotetraploidy in miscanthus.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18923-6/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18923_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18923-6/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18923_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="613" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Syntenic relationships between sorghum and <i>M. sinensis</i> subgenomes MsA and MsB. Distribution of subgenome-specific 13-mer sequences (blue for MsA, red for MsB) is shown for each <i>M. sinensis</i> chromosome (see text and Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">7.1</a>). <b>b</b> Clustering of counts of 13-mers that differentiate homeologous chromosomes enables the consistent partitioning of the genome into two subgenomes. Blue chromosome names correspond to the A subgenome, red chromosome names correspond to the B subgenome. <b>c</b> Timetree of Andropogoneae showing the timeline of allotetraploidy in the <i>Miscanthus</i> lineage, with divergence and hybridization times of the A and B progenitors estimated from sequence comparisons (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">8</a>). Source Data underlying Fig. 1b are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Miscanthus–Saccharum synteny."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: <i>Miscanthus</i>–<i>Saccharum</i> synteny.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18923-6/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18923_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18923-6/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18923_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="454" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Dotplot showing co-orthologs between <i>Miscanthus sinensis</i> and <i>Saccharum spontaneum</i>. All syntenic genes with c score ≥ 0.7 are shown using the mcscan ortholog algorithm. Note the 2:4 ratio of miscanthus:sugarcane chromosome segments. Source Data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Regarding the more than twofold difference in bulk genome size between sorghum and miscanthus, we find that lengths of coding sequence and introns are generally similar (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">4b, c</a>), with overall differences arising from increased intergenic spacing in miscanthus due to transposon insertion, as well as by the expansion of repetitive pericentromeric regions, which are only partially captured in the assembly (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">4b</a>). The chromatin conformation contact map (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">2a</a>) exhibits an enrichment of centromeric and telomeric contacts, respectively, consistent with the interphase nuclear “Rabl” conformation as seen in the barley genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Mascher, M. et al. A chromosome conformation capture ordered sequence of the barley genome. Nature 544, 427–433 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1766">21</a></sup>. We identified locally interacting chromosomal compartments (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">2b</a> and Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">2</a>) for which A compartments have a higher gene density and B compartments have lower gene density (one-sided <i>t</i>-test <i>p</i> value &lt; 2.2 × 10<sup>−16</sup>) and tend to occur predominantly in the pericentromeric region, as observed in other plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Dong, P. et al. 3D chromatin architecture of large plant genomes determined by local A/B compartments. Mol. Plant 10, 1497–1509 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1785">22</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Allotetraploid origin of <i>Miscanthus</i>
</h3><p>An allotetraploid (i.e., hybrid) origin for a paleotetraploid species is commonly demonstrated by showing that one set of its chromosomes (a subgenome) is more closely related to some diploid lineages to the exclusion of others<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Edger, P. P., McKain, M. R., Bird, K. A. &amp; VanBuren, R. Subgenome assignment in allopolyploids: challenges and future directions. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 42, 76–80 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1801">23</a></sup>. Because there are no known candidates for the diploid progenitors of tetraploid miscanthus, this approach cannot be used here. Instead, we used a new method that relies on the chromosomal distribution of repetitive elements, which can provide robust markers for subgenome ancestry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Session, A. M. et al. Genome evolution in the allotetraploid frog Xenopus laevis. Nature 538, 336–343 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1805">24</a></sup>. We sought repetitive sequences whose presence is enriched on one member of each homeologous chromosome pair (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">6</a>). Such sequences are definitive markers of allotetraploidy, and occur as relicts of repetitive elements that were active in only one of the two diploid progenitors prior to hybridization and genome doubling<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Session, A. M. et al. Genome evolution in the allotetraploid frog Xenopus laevis. Nature 538, 336–343 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1812">24</a></sup>. Importantly, the method does not require access to or even knowledge of living representatives of the progenitor lineages. We found 1187 13-bp sequences (13-mers) whose pairwise enrichment pattern consistently partitions homeologous chromosome pairs between distinct A and B subgenomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig1">1a, b</a>). This observation establishes the past existence of distinct A and B progenitor lineages (which remained separate for millions of years, see below), and the allotetraploid origin of miscanthus.</p><p>Although we can use these markers to assign each miscanthus chromosome in bulk to the A or B subgenome, we find evidence for the balanced reciprocal exchange of distal segments between homeologous chromosomes such that dosage remains intact (e.g., the ends of chromosomes 5–6, 11–12, and 16–17; Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig1">1</a>a, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig3">3a</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">6</a>, and Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">6</a>). Based on consistency with our dense genetic map, these are clearly bona fide homeologous exchanges rather than misassemblies. The observed distal reciprocal exchanges likely occurred either by mitotic recombination in the vegetative tissue of an AB F1 hybrid founder prior to genome doubling, or by aberrant homeologous recombination after allotetraploidy. The concentration of these exchanges toward the ends of chromosomes is consistent with the proximity of these regions in a telomeric bouquet conformation. The maintenance of discrete A/B patterns of diagnostic 13-mers in these distal segments implies that these exchanges occurred by single crossover events rather than recurring recombination throughout the distal regions of the chromosomes, which would blur the distinctive A/B 13-mer signature.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Post-allotetraploidy reciprocal exchanges."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Post-allotetraploidy reciprocal exchanges.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18923-6/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18923_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18923-6/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18923_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="448" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Example of a chromosome pair without reciprocal exchange (chr01–chr02), and two chromosome pairs with distal reciprocal exchanges (chr05–chr06 and chr11–chr12). Red and blue dots represent occurrences of subgenome-specific 13-mers. Black bars identify A and B ancestry inferred from a Hidden Markov Model (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">7.2</a>). <b>b</b> Relative expression of homeologous gene pairs. Across tissues and seasonal sampling times, there is a 3.8% median bias toward the expression of the B member of the pair. <b>c</b> Homeologous gene pairs within reciprocally exchanged regions show the expression bias of their ancestral location. Source Data underlying Fig. 3b, c are provided in the Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Discrete homeologous exchanges are often observed in newly formed allotetraploids and are thought to occur in response to a new meiotic environment<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Xiong, Z., Gaeta, R. T. &amp; Pires, J. C. Homoeologous shuffling and chromosome compensation maintain genome balance in resynthesized allopolyploid Brassica napus. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. U.S.A. 108, 7908–7913 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1868">25</a></sup>. In studies of other polyploids, homeologous replacements that alter the balance between A and B alleles are common; when such variants are segregating in a population, the resulting genetic variation can underlie quantitative trait loci<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Stein, A. et al. Mapping of homoeologous chromosome exchanges influencing quantitative trait variation in Brassica napus. Plant Biotechnol. J. 15, 1478–1489 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1872">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wu, D. et al. Whole-genome resequencing of a worldwide collection of rapeseed accessions reveals the genetic basis of ecotype divergence. Mol. Plant 12, 30–43 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1875">27</a></sup>. In contrast to these studies, however, in <i>Miscanthus</i>, we find (1) predominantly balanced reciprocal exchanges that alter chromosomal linkage, but do not change A/B dosage, and (2) no evidence that these segmental exchanges are segregating in our sequenced samples, suggesting that the reciprocal homeologous exchanges are the result of ancient events that have become fixed in <i>Miscanthus</i> (and therefore cannot be causal for any phenotypic variation in the genus) (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">6</a>)). In addition to these long fixed reciprocal exchanges, there are several shorter internal homeologous segments (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">6</a>) that could correspond to nonreciprocal or recurrent exchange. These segments will be interesting to study further.</p><p>From the identification of distinct A and B subgenomes, we see that the sorghum-7 and -4-like chromosomes that fused<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Swaminathan, K. et al. A framework genetic map for Miscanthus sinensis from RNAseq-based markers shows recent tetraploidy. BMC Genom. 13, 142 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1895">8</a></sup> to form miscanthus chromosome 7 were both derived from the B progenitor. While it is possible that the fusion occurred in the B progenitor itself prior to hybridization, the absence of other Saccharinae with <i>n</i> = 9 chromosomes, and the likelihood of chromosome instability in the aftermath of allotetraploidization, suggests that the fusion occurred after allohybridization.</p><p>The timeline of paleotetraploidy in miscanthus can be established through inter- and intra-subgenome comparisons (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig1">1c</a> and Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">7</a>). We estimate that the A and B progenitors diverged from their common ancestor ~7.2 Mya (million years ago), based on the synonymous differences between homeologous protein-coding genes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">7</a>). After this divergence but before hybridization, the two (now likely extinct) progenitors evolved independently; evidence of their species-specific transposable element activity appears in the contemporary <i>Miscanthus</i> genome as subgenome-specific repeats<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Session, A. M. et al. Genome evolution in the allotetraploid frog Xenopus laevis. Nature 538, 336–343 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1918">24</a></sup>. Consistent with this hypothesis, we find several LTR-retrotransposon families within only one of the two subgenomes, and estimate that they were actively inserting during the period ~2.5–6 Mya (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">7</a>). In contrast, transposon activity after the allotetraploidy event should be distributed across the entire <i>Miscanthus</i> genome without regard to subgenomes. Also, consistent with this picture, we find a burst of transposon activity that is not subgenome-specific starting ~2.5 Mya, which serves as our best estimate for the allotetraploid origin of <i>Miscanthus</i> (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">7</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">7c</a>). Finally, the interfertile sister species <i>M. sinensis</i> and <i>M. sacchariflorus</i> diverged ~1.65 Mya (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig1">1c</a>), consistent with speciation occurring after allotetraploidy. Chromosome-level comparisons of repetitive elements and protein sequences confirm that the polyploidies of <i>Miscanthus</i> and sugarcane occurred independently (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">7</a>).</p><p>Common hallmarks of allopolyploidy are asymmetric gene loss (or conversely, retention) and biased gene expression between subgenomes, which are both thought to arise from epigenetic asymmetries in the aftermath of allohybridization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. U. S. A. 108, 4069–4074 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1957">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Garsmeur, O. et al. Two evolutionarily distinct classes of paleopolyploidy. Mol. Biol. Evol. 31, 448–454 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1960">29</a></sup>. Comparing miscanthus and sorghum genes, we find that ~29% of sorghum genes have been lost on one of two subgenomes; conversely, ~71% have co-orthologs on both subgenomes (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">6</a>). Gene retention in <i>M. sinensis</i> shows a small but statistically significant bias toward the B subgenome (87.1% genes retained on B vs. 83.9% on A, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">7</a>; Fisher’s exact <i>p</i> value, two-sided = 1.2 × 10<sup>−9</sup>). The level of homeologous gene retention in <i>M. sinensis</i> is nearly twice that of maize (71% vs. 36%), presumably because the miscanthus allotetraploidy is more recent. The subgenome retention bias in <i>Miscanthus</i> is also smaller than in maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. U. S. A. 108, 4069–4074 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d34750e1985">28</a></sup> (80.6% in maize 1 vs. 55.4% in maize 2), which may reflect differences in the degree of genomic differentiation between maize versus <i>Miscanthus</i> progenitors prior to hybridization.</p><p>Similarly, for retained homeolog pairs, we find a weak but significant expression bias (median B/A expression ratio 1.038, without strong variation across tissues or season, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig3">3b</a>). Although most pairs of homeologous genes have similar expression levels, there are ~10% more pairs with higher B-subgenome expression than vice versa (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">8</a>). This is again notably weaker than the expression bias in maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. U. S. A. 108, 4069–4074 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2001">28</a></sup>. Interestingly, genes in regions of homeologous exchange show (on average) the bias of their source subgenome (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">8</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig3">3c</a>), indicating that subgenome expression bias arises from local effects and/or became fixed early in the allotetraploid evolution. This observation is consistent with experiments that show rapid development of subgenome bias in neoallopolyploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Xiong, Z., Gaeta, R. T. &amp; Pires, J. C. Homoeologous shuffling and chromosome compensation maintain genome balance in resynthesized allopolyploid Brassica napus. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. U.S.A. 108, 7908–7913 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2012">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Adams, K. L., Cronn, R., Percifield, R. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Genes duplicated by polyploidy show unequal contributions to the transcriptome and organ-specific reciprocal silencing. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. U. S. A. 100, 4649–4654 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2015">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Subgenome dominance in an interspecific hybrid, synthetic allopolyploid, and a 140-year-old naturally established neo-allopolyploid monkeyflower. Plant Cell 29, 2150–2167 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2018">31</a></sup>. The weaker subgenome expression and retention bias seen in the more recent miscanthus allotetraploidy versus the older maize suggests that these effects may become amplified over time, and may also be influenced by the relative genomic divergence of progenitors.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Seasonal dynamics of gene expression</h3><p>As a rhizomatous perennial, miscanthus provides a model for studying the biology of rhizomes, which are modified underground stems that enable temperate perennial grasses to overwinter by their capacity to (1) store nitrogen, carbon, and other nutrients from senescing leaves and stems, and (2) mobilize these reserves in the spring to feed new vegetative growth. Amino acids, particularly asparagine with its high N:C ratio, are the primary form of nitrogen cycled among plant tissues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Urquhart, A. A. &amp; Joy, K. W. Use of Phloem exudate technique in the study of amino acid transport in pea plants. Plant Physiol. 68, 750–754 (1981)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2030">32</a></sup>. Monitoring free asparagine concentrations (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig4">4a</a>) from stem, leaf, and rhizome tissues of <i>M. × giganteus</i> sampled throughout the growing season (May to October) over 3 years revealed high concentrations in the spring rhizome, low levels in all tissues during the summer period of rapid growth, followed by increasing accumulation in stem and rhizomes after flowering. Elevated asparagine levels mark periods of active nitrogen remobilization from rhizome to shoot in spring, and from the shoot to rhizome in autumn.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Seasonal gene expression changes in miscanthus."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Seasonal gene expression changes in miscanthus.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18923-6/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18923_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18923-6/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18923_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="664" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Shows asparagine in rhizome, stem, and leaves over the growing seasons normalized to total nitrogen in the sample. The error bars represent the standard deviation. <b>b</b> Principal component analysis of RNA-seq read counts normalized using the DESeq2 variance-stabilizing transformation method. PC1/2 distinguishes the three tissues from each other. <b>c</b> PC3/PC4 separates samples based on their nutrient mobilization status. The color scheme for the organs and dates matches <b>b</b>. <b>d</b> Heatmap across all tissues in the study comparing the expression of a subset of genes expressed in tissues that are actively remobilizing nutrients. Source Data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To characterize the seasonal dynamics of gene expression and regulatory programs associated with perenniality in <i>Miscanthus</i>, we performed RNA-seq from the same tissue samples collected for profiling nitrogen cycling (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">8</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM5">1</a>). Principal component analysis (PCA) identified the two largest sources of variation as tissue type, followed by sampling time (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig4">4b</a>). Comparisons among tissues produced a catalog of organ-preferred genes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">8</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM5">1</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM13">9</a>). As expected, leaf-preferred genes are significantly enriched in genes functioning in carbon fixation and metabolism, and stem-preferred genes include those associated with phenylpropanoid biosynthesis and amino acid metabolism. Gene expression in rhizomes is more similar to stems than leaves, consistent with their developmental origin as modified stems (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">8a, b</a>). Relative to stems and leaves, rhizomes preferentially express transcription factors that regulate growth and metabolic processes, and genes that respond to stimuli such as water and stress (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">8e</a>). We identified 35 genes that are preferentially expressed in the rhizome, including homologs of genes like <i>GIANT KILLER</i> (<i>GIK</i>) and <i>SHORT INTERNODE</i> (<i>SHI</i>) implicated in organ patterning, differentiation, and cell elongation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ng, K.-H., Yu, H. &amp; Ito, T. AGAMOUS controls GIANT KILLER, a multifunctional chromatin modifier in reproductive organ patterning and differentiation. PLoS Biol. 7, e1000251 (2009)." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2118">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ng, K.-H. &amp; Ito, T. Shedding light on the role of AT-hook/PPC domain protein in Arabidopsis thaliana. Plant Signal. Behav. 5, 200–201 (2010)." href="#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2118_1">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Fridborg, I., Kuusk, S., Moritz, T. &amp; Sundberg, E. The Arabidopsis dwarf mutant shi exhibits reduced gibberellin responses conferred by overexpression of a new putative zinc finger protein. Plant Cell 11, 1019–1032 (1999)." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2118_2">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Topp, S. H. &amp; Rasmussen, S. K. A survey of shitranscription factors across plant species and their application in horticulture. Acta Hortic. 974, 149–156 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2121">36</a></sup>. Overexpression of <i>SHI</i>-like genes results in compact plants with shorter stem internodes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Islam, M. A. et al. Overexpression of the AtSHI gene in poinsettia, Euphorbia pulcherrima, results in compact plants. PLoS One 8, e53377 (2013)." href="#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2128">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zawaski, C. et al. SHORT INTERNODES-like genes regulate shoot growth and xylem proliferation in Populus. N. Phytol. 191, 678–691 (2011)." href="#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2128_1">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Lütken, H. et al. Production of compact plants by overexpression of AtSHI in the ornamental Kalanchoë. Plant Biotechnol. J. 8, 211–222 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2131">39</a></sup>, which is consistent with the morphological differences between miscanthus rhizomes and stems.</p><p>We identified and characterized the transcriptional network regulating seasonal nutrient mobilization in miscanthus (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">8</a>), which is central to the perennial lifecycle and efficient recycling of resources. Although tissue identity dominates the first two principal components of gene expression, the third component (PC3) separates the spring rhizomes, fall leaves, and fall stems from the other tissues (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig4">4c</a>). Differentially expressed genes contributing to the pattern in PC3 (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">8</a>) comprise a dynamic network differentiating the fall rhizome that is storing nitrogen from the spring rhizomes that are releasing nitrogen to promote new growth (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM5">1</a>). Of these genes, 104 had a functional or KEGG assignment, including a suite of transcription factors and genes with known important roles in nitrogen mobilization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Havé, M., Marmagne, A., Chardon, F. &amp; Masclaux-Daubresse, C. Nitrogen remobilization during leaf senescence: lessons from Arabidopsis to crops. J. Exp. Bot. 68, 2513–2529 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2150">40</a></sup> like <i>ASPARAGINE SYNTHETASE</i> (<i>ASN1</i>), <i>GLUTAMATE DEHYDROGENASE</i> (<i>GDH2</i>), and <i>GLUTAMATE DECARBOXYLASE</i> (<i>GAD1</i>). Remarkably, the most prominent (“hubby” or central) transcription factors within the network are a subset of JASMONATE ZIM DOMAIN (JAZ) family proteins that regulate jasmonic acid biosynthesis (e.g., ALLENE OXIDE SYNTHASE, AOS) and signaling, a pathway recently shown to activate nitrogen remobilization in rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Wu, X. et al. The roles of jasmonate signalling in nitrogen uptake and allocation in rice (Oryza sativa L.). Plant, Cell Environ. 42, 659–672 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2174">41</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig4">4d</a>). These data reveal a group of regulators and enzymes that may be key for promoting the nitrogen remobilization in spring.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Inter- and intraspecific variation and introgression</h3><p>Breeding to improve miscanthus for biomass and other applications can draw upon extensive wild germplasm from multiple species and ploidy levels. We therefore investigated the genetic diversity of <i>Miscanthus</i> and the distribution of inter- and intraspecific variation in admixed populations. We combined new WGS sequencing of 18 accessions of varying ploidy, including the triploid biofuel cultivar <i>M</i>. <i>× giganteus</i> “Illinois” (see Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">9</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">9</a>) with previously generated genotyping-by-sequencing data from primarily wild accessions with broad geographic coverage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Clark, L. V. et al. Genetic structure of Miscanthus sinensis and Miscanthus sacchariflorus in Japan indicates a gradient of bidirectional but asymmetric introgression. J. Exp. Bot. 66, 4213–4225 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2205">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Clark, L. V. et al. Population structure of Miscanthus sacchariflorus reveals two major polyploidization events, tetraploid-mediated unidirectional introgression from diploid M. sinensis, and diversity centred around the Yellow Sea. Ann. Bot. &#xA;                  https://academic.oup.com/aob/advance-article-abstract/doi/10.1093/aob/mcy161/5104475&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2208">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Clark, L. V. et al. A footprint of past climate change on the diversity and population structure of Miscanthus sinensis. Ann. Bot. 114, 97–107 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2211">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Clark, L. V. et al. Ecological characteristics and in situ genetic associations for yield-component traits of wild Miscanthus from eastern Russia. Ann. Bot. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/aob/mcw137&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2214">43</a></sup>, spanning the native range of miscanthus across north- and south-east China, Korea, Russia, and Japan. Genome-wide admixture (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig5">5a</a>) and PCA (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig5">5b</a>) readily differentiate two species, <i>M. sinensis</i> and <i>M. sacchariflorus</i>. Other named <i>Miscanthus</i> accessions, such <i>M</i>. <i>transmorrisonensis</i> and <i>M. floridulus</i>, lie within the range of genetic variation of <i>M. sinensis</i>, suggesting that these taxa should more properly be considered subtypes of <i>M. sinensis</i>. The accession in our collection named <i>Miscanthus junceus</i>, however, is clearly distinct and appears to be more closely related to sugarcanes than <i>Miscanthus</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">9</a>). It is African, sometimes classified in a separate genus <i>Miscanthidium</i>, and clearly separate from <i>Miscanthus</i> sensu stricto<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Hodkinson, T. R. Characterization of a genetic resource collection for Miscanthus (Saccharinae, Andropogoneae, Poaceae) using AFLP and ISSR PCR. Ann. Bot. 89, 627–636 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2265">44</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Miscanthus population structure and segmental ancestry."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: <i>Miscanthus</i> population structure and segmental ancestry.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18923-6/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18923_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18923-6/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18923_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="434" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b> Population structure of 407 miscanthus accessions, including 57 <i>M</i>. × <i>giganteus</i>, 120 <i>M. sacchariflorus</i> (<i>Msa</i>), and <i>M. sinensis</i> (<i>Msi</i>) from China (75), Korea (15), and Japan (140). <b>b</b> Principal component analysis of 407 miscanthus accessions where “x” marks admixtures of <i>Msa</i> and <i>Msi</i>. Such hybrids are collectively referred to as <i>M</i>. × <i>giganteus</i> (Mxg), and can be diploid, triploid, or tetraploid. Separation of Japanese and mainland Asian populations is largely consistent with structure analysis in <b>a</b>. Whole-genome shotgun (WGS)-sequenced accessions are labeled. <b>c</b> Segmental ancestry of miscanthus accessions based on WGS sequencing. Each horizontal bar denotes one (imputed) haploid chromosome set; red and blue indicate <i>Msa</i> and <i>Msi</i> ancestry, respectively. The number of bars represents ploidy. Introgression of <i>M. sacchariflorus</i> into <i>M. sinensis</i> (MsiEF148, Undine, DH2, DH2P) is common among cultivated European types (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">10</a>). Source Data underlying Fig. 5c are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Our chromosome-scale genome assembly allows us to investigate patterns of admixture in interspecific hybrids (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig5">5c</a>). While all <i>M. sinensis</i> × <i>M. sacchariflorus</i> hybrids and admixtures are taxonomically characterized as <i>M</i>. × <i>giganteus</i>, this nothospecies has rich diversity due to the occurrence of diploid, triploid, and tetraploid accessions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">10</a>). We find that many ornamental diploids, especially many bred by Ernst Pagels in Germany, contain chromosomal segments of <i>M. sacchariflorus</i> introgressed into an <i>M. sinensis</i> background, consistent with prior admixture studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Clark, L. V. et al. Genetic structure of Miscanthus sinensis and Miscanthus sacchariflorus in Japan indicates a gradient of bidirectional but asymmetric introgression. J. Exp. Bot. 66, 4213–4225 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2379">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Clark, L. V. et al. Population structure of Miscanthus sacchariflorus reveals two major polyploidization events, tetraploid-mediated unidirectional introgression from diploid M. sinensis, and diversity centred around the Yellow Sea. Ann. Bot. &#xA;                  https://academic.oup.com/aob/advance-article-abstract/doi/10.1093/aob/mcy161/5104475&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2382">12</a></sup>. Mainland Asian and Japanese <i>M. sinensis</i> are distinct subpopulations (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig5">5a</a>) that diverged ~500,000–1000,000 years ago based on chloroplast DNA (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">9</a>).</p><p>Our data confirm that the highly productive triploid biofuel <i>M</i>. × <i>giganteus</i> genotype, “Illinois,” is an interspecific hybrid of tetraploid <i>M. sacchariflorus</i> and diploid <i>M. sinensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Głowacka, K. et al. Genetic variation in Miscanthus× giganteus and the importance of estimating genetic distance thresholds for differentiating clones. GCB Bioenergy 7, 386–404 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2410">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Hodkinson, T. R. et al. The use of DNA sequencing (ITS and trnL-F), AFLP, and fluorescent in situ hybridization to study allopolyploid Miscanthus (Poaceae). Am. J. Bot. 89, 279–286 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2413">45</a></sup>. We find a predominant 2:1 ratio of <i>M. sacchariflorus</i>: <i>M. sinensis</i> alleles across the entire genome, consistent with this hypothesis; however, we also observed that the <i>M. sacchariflorus</i> ancestor had interspecific admixture (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig5">5c</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">10c, e</a>), which indicates that the most productive miscanthus genotype currently grown is the product of more than one cycle of introgression from <i>M. sinensis</i> into <i>M. sacchariflorus</i>. Hybrids between <i>M. sacchariflorus</i> and <i>M. sinensis</i> are frequently highly vigorous and high-yielding, regardless of whether they are diploid, triploid, or tetraploid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Clark, L. V. et al. Genome‐wide association and genomic prediction for biomass yield in a genetically diverse Miscanthus sinensis germplasm panel phenotyped at five locations in Asia and North America. GCB Bioenergy 8, 585 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2446">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Dong, H. et al. Winter hardiness of Miscanthus (I): overwintering ability and yield of new Miscanthus × giganteus genotypes in Illinois and Arkansas. GCB Bioenergy 11, 691–705 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2449">47</a></sup>. Thus, understanding how prior introgression of <i>M. sinensis</i> alleles into a primarily <i>M. sacchariflorus</i> genetic background affects the yield potential of subsequent interspecific hybrids will be important for optimizing breeding strategies. In particular, <i>M</i>. × <i>giganteus</i> combines the tufted habit (many stems per area; short rhizomes) of its <i>M. sinensis</i> parent with the spreading rhizomatous habit (few stems per area; long rhizomes) of its <i>M. sacchariflorus</i> parent, typically in an intermediate form, and optimizing the number of stems per area is critical to breeding for high yield in <i>M</i>. × <i>giganteus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Matumura, M., Hasegawa, T. &amp; Saijoh, Y. Ecological aspects of Miscanthus sinensis var. condensatus, M. sacchariflorus and their 3×-, 4×-hybrids (2) Growth behaviour of the current year’s rhizomes. Research Bulletin of the Faculty of Agriculture, Gifu University 51, 347–362 (1986)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2477">48</a></sup>. The recently collected Japanese <i>M</i>. × <i>giganteus</i> triploid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Nishiwaki, A. et al. Discovery of natural Miscanthus (Poaceae) triploid plants in sympatric populations of Miscanthus sacchariflorus and Miscanthus sinensis in southern Japan. Am. J. Bot. 98, 154–159 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2488">49</a></sup> “Ogi80” has a similar pattern to “Illinois,” with both including several short blocks containing two or three <i>M. sinensis</i> alleles. These regions could be due to segmental gene conversion or loss during the propagation of this sterile triploid, or interspecific introgression prior to triploid formation.
