Another natural triploid, “Ogi63,” shows a distinct pattern, highlighting the diversity of natural polyploid <i>Miscanthus</i> hybrids (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">10</a>).</p><p>Miscanthus is a promising perennial biomass source and candidate biofuel crop with efficient C4 photosynthesis that is highly adaptable. Its ability to grow on marginal lands with limited inputs, and its high drought and chilling tolerance make it suitable for both tropical and temperate climates. The genome sequence and genomic analysis presented here provides a foundation for systematic improvement of <i>Miscanthus</i> to optimize its productivity and robustness. Comparative analyses among the Andropogoneae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Hodkinson, T. R. Evolution and taxonomy of the grasses (Poaceae): a model family for the study of species-rich groups. Annu. Plant Rev. Online 1–39 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2507">50</a></sup>, which unites miscanthus with maize, sorghum, and sugarcane, promise to reveal the genetic basis for innovations that contribute to the high productivity and wide adaptation of this tribe of grasses.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Genome sequencing and chromosomal assembly</h3><p>We shotgun-sequenced the <i>M. sinensis</i> genome at ~90× redundancy with Illumina paired-end and mate-pair data, augmented by fosmid-end pairs and in vitro and in vivo chromatin conformation capture (HiC) as described in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">1</a>. Illumina shotgun assembly was performed with Meraculous2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Chapman, J. A., Ho, I. Y., Goltsman, E. &amp; Rokhsar, D. S. Meraculous2: fast accurate short-read assembly of large polymorphic genomes. (2016). &#xA;                  http://arxiv.org/abs/1608.01031&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2530">51</a></sup> and organized into chromosomes with HiC data using HiRise (Dovetail Genomics, Scotts Valley, CA) followed by manual curation with Juicebox<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Durand, N. C. et al. Juicebox provides a visualization system for Hi-C contact maps with unlimited zoom. Cell Syst. 3, 99–101 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2534">52</a></sup>, and confirmation of internal self-consistency as described in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">2</a>. The assembly was further corroborated and assigned to chromosomes using a genetic map derived from four crosses, with 4298 uniquely assignable 64-bp markers, as described in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">3</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Protein-coding gene and transposable element annotation</h3><p>Protein-coding gene structures were annotated using the DOE Joint Genome Institute annotation pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Simakov, O. et al. Insights into bilaterian evolution from three spiralian genomes. Nature 493, 526–531 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2553">53</a></sup> that incorporates transcriptional evidence, homology support from related grasses, and ab initio methods, as described in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">4</a>. RNA-seq data from three tissues and 57 timepoints for <i>M</i>. × <i>giganteus</i> and <i>M. sinensis</i> DH1 leaf and rhizome (PRJNA575573, SRP017791) were used, and these data are summarized in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">8</a> including accession numbers. Genome completeness was estimated using BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2573">54</a></sup>, and orthologous gene families identified using OrthoVenn<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Xu, L. et al. OrthoVenn2: a web server for whole-genome comparison and annotation of orthologous clusters across multiple species. Nucleic Acids Res. 47, W52–W58 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2577">55</a></sup> as described in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">4</a>.</p><p>Transposable elements were identified de novo using RepeatModeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Smit, A. F. A. &amp; Hubley, R. RepeatModeler Open-1.0 &#xA;                  http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2587">56</a></sup> to augment existing catalogs of grass repeats from repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Bao, W., Kojima, K. K. &amp; Kohany, O. Repbase Update, a database of repetitive elements in eukaryotic genomes. Mob. DNA 6, 11 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2591">57</a></sup> and MIPS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Nussbaumer, T. et al. MIPS PlantsDB: a database framework for comparative plant genome research. Nucleic Acids Res. 41, D1144–D1151 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2595">58</a></sup> using RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Smit, A. F. A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker Open-4.0 &#xA;                  http://www.repeatmasker.org/RMDownload.html&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2599">59</a></sup>, and identified intact retrotransposons with LTRHarvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinform. 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2603">60</a></sup>, as described in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">5</a>. LTR families were defined by clustering these LTRs with those of sorghum and sugarcane by BLAST score using 90% identity and 90% length cutoffs as described in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">5</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Subgenome and homeologous exchange identification</h3><p>We partitioned the <i>M. sinensis</i> genome into subgenomes A and B by a modification of methods described in Session et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Session, A. M. et al. Genome evolution in the allotetraploid frog Xenopus laevis. Nature 538, 336–343 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2625">24</a></sup> and described more fully in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">6</a>. Importantly, this method can be applied without requiring sequences from extant A and B diploids. Briefly, we identified 1187 13-bp sequences (13-mers) that (1) occurred at least 100 times across the genome, and (2) were at least twofold enriched in one member of each homeologous chromosome pair (excluding the case of fused homeologs). 13-mers were counted using Jellyfish<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Marçais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764–770 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2632">61</a></sup>. Homeologous chromosomes were determined based on conserved synteny to each other and to sorghum (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig1">1</a>). These 13-mers allowed chromosomes to be clustered by subgenome, and were found to overlap with subgenome-specific repeats as described in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">6</a>. To identify cases of homeologous exchanges, we sought chromosomal regions whose 13-mer identity differed from the overall identify of the chromosome, using a hidden Markov model whose observed state was the number of A- and B-specific 13-mers and whose emitted state is A or B, as described in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">6</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Determination of biases in subgenome gene retention</h3><p>We used two methods to determine orthology between <i>M. sinensis</i> genes and sorghum in order to assess differential retention of gene duplicates after allotetraploidy, using sorghum as the outgroup representing the ancestral (preduplicated state). For the first method, gene families were constructed using OrthoVenn<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Xu, L. et al. OrthoVenn2: a web server for whole-genome comparison and annotation of orthologous clusters across multiple species. Nucleic Acids Res. 47, W52–W58 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2657">55</a></sup>. For the second method, we used BLAST-based clustering. Subgenome-specific retention is defined as the number of genes on a given subgenome divided by the number of inferred ancestral (i.e., preduplication) gene number. Details of this analysis can be found in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">6</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Timing of events associated with allotetraploidy</h3><p>We estimated the timing of speciations in the Andropogoneae using a set of 1:1 orthologs for species shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig1">1c</a> with <i>P. hallii</i> and <i>S. italica</i> as outgroups, as described in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">7</a>. Briefly, concatenated multiple-sequence alignments were produced using Dialign-TX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Subramanian, A. R., Kaufmann, M. &amp; Morgenstern, B. DIALIGN-TX: greedy and progressive approaches for segment-based multiple sequence alignment. Algorithms Mol. Biol. 3, 6 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2685">62</a></sup> and Gblocks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Talavera, G. &amp; Castresana, J. Improvement of phylogenies after removing divergent and ambiguously aligned blocks from protein sequence alignments. Syst. Biol. 56, 564–577 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2690">63</a></sup>. <i>M. sinensis</i> and maize genes were partitioned into A and B subgenomes, and 1 and 2 subgenomes, respectively, with 1–2 assignments as determined by Schnable et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. U. S. A. 108, 4069–4074 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2697">28</a></sup>. The dataset included <i>M. sacchariflorus</i> A and B genes predicted by mapping diploid <i>M. sacchariflorus</i> shotgun sequence to the <i>M. sinensis</i> assembly. <i>M. sacchariflorus</i> has the same karyotype as <i>M. sinensis</i>, and hybrids are fertile, indicating that they share the same A/B ancestral tetraploidy. Phylogenies were produced from the resulting 28,887 nucleotide alignment using PhyML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Guindon, S. et al. New algorithms and methods to estimate maximum-likelihood phylogenies: assessing the performance of PhyML 3.0. Syst. Biol. 59, 307–321 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2717">64</a></sup>. Timetrees were estimated using r8s<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Sanderson, M. J. r8s: inferring absolute rates of molecular evolution and divergence times in the absence of a molecular clock. Bioinformatics 19, 301–302 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2721">65</a></sup> with a smoothing parameter of 0.1, and constraining the <i>Setaria</i>/<i>Panicum</i> node to 12.8–20 Mya and the <i>Sorghum</i>/maize split to 13–21.2 Mya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Christin, P.-A. et al. Oligocene CO2 decline promoted C4 photosynthesis in grasses. Curr. Biol. 18, 37–43 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2735">66</a></sup>.</p><p>We estimated the period during which the A and B progenitors were separate species using phylogenies of five subgenome-specific LTR families with ≥100 members that contain a subgenome-enriched 13-mer, as described in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">7</a>. Subgenome-specific LTR families have been active when the two progenitors were separate species, but before allotetraploidy. To calibrate the rate of LTR substitution in miscanthus, we used LTR families that are (1) found in high copy number in miscanthus across both the A and B subgenomes, and so were active after allotetraploidy, and (2) have parallel activity in the sorghum genome, and used a miscanthus–sorghum divergence time of 10 My as determined from protein-coding genes. We used the median substitution rate of these families (2.1 × 10<sup>−8</sup> substitutions per My) to infer the timing of subgenome-specific activity based on Jukes–Cantor distance. Details are provided in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">7</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Analysis of gene expression</h3><p>We analyzed RNA-seq data using Tophat2.1.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Kim, D. et al. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 14, R36 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2758">67</a></sup>, HTSeq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Anders, S., Pyl, P. T. &amp; Huber, W. HTSeq-a Python framework to work with high-throughput sequencing data. Bioinformatics 31, 166–169 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2762">68</a></sup>, DESeq2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Love, M. I., Huber, W. &amp; Anders, S. Moderated estimation of fold change and dispersion for RNA-seq data with DESeq2. Genome Biol. 15, 550 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2766">69</a></sup>, and the NOISeq R package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Tarazona, S., García-Alcalde, F., Dopazo, J., Ferrer, A. &amp; Conesa, A. Differential expression in RNA-seq: a matter of depth. Genome Res. 21, 2213–2223 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2770">70</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Tarazona, S. et al. Data quality aware analysis of differential expression in RNA-seq with NOISeq R/Bioc package. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, e140 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2773">71</a></sup> to extract expression levels and further analyze the RNA-seq data as described in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">8</a>. To identify genes that were constitutively expressed in any one organ type, we considered only genes with a count per million (cpm) of 5 or greater within all samples of an organ type. KEGG enrichment analysis using keggseq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Korani, W., Chu, Y., Holbrook, C. C. &amp; Ozias-Akins, P. Insight into genes regulating postharvest Aflatoxin contamination of tetraploid peanut from transcriptional profiling. Genetics 209, 143–156 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2781">72</a></sup> was performed on genes that were preferentially in leaves, stems, and rhizomes, respectively, to determine if they clustered into specific pathways or functional categories. Enriched pathways with a <i>q</i> value ≤ 0.01 are shown in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">8c–e</a>.</p><p>For the purposes of comparing gene expression of homeologs, we measured gene expression using cpm, after combining replicates, as described in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">8</a>. In order to measure subgenome expression bias, for each homeolog pair, we considered only experiments where one or both homeologs have nonzero expression (cpm &gt; 0.5). This condition is necessary because the majority of genes are not expressed in every tissue, leading to a large number of uninformative comparisons. We considered expression bias using a variant of the approach of Schnable et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. U. S. A. 108, 4069–4074 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2797">28</a></sup>, identifying homeolog pairs where one member of the pair was expressed <i>X</i>-fold relative to the other, where <i>X</i> = 2, 5, and 10, again requiring both members to be expressed at a minimal level (cpm &gt; 0.5) to avoid uninformative comparisons.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Analysis of genetic variation</h3><p>WGS sequences of 18 miscanthus accessions (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">9</a>) were aligned to the haploid <i>M. sinensis</i> DH1 reference sequence using bwa mem<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 26, 589–595 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2821">73</a></sup>, and variants called using GATK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2825">74</a></sup> version 3.6, as described in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">9</a>. Restriction site-associated DNA-sequencing (RAD-seq) data from 2819 <i>Miscanthus</i> individuals were used to obtain a snapshot of genetic diversity, as described in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">9</a>.</p><p>For PCA with the RAD-seq data genotypes, we retained SNPs with a maximum of 30% missing data and a minimum minor allele frequency of 0.01, resulting in a set of 144,337 SNPs. From this dataset, individuals with 50% or more missing data were removed, leaving 2492 out of the original 2819 individuals. By filtering SNPs and individuals in this way, the remaining data were primarily derived from <i>Pst</i>I sequencing libraries, as this was the enzyme most commonly used across the dataset. Genotypes were coded on a numeric scale from 0 to 1, indicating copy number for the nonreference allele, i.e., 0, 0.5, and 1 for diploids, 0. 0.33, 0.67, and 1 for triploids, and 0, 0.25, 0.5, 0.75, and 1 for tetraploids. PCA was performed using probabilistic PCA method implemented in the Bioconductor package pcaMethods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Stacklies, W., Redestig, H., Scholz, M., Walther, D. &amp; Selbig, J. pcaMethods-a bioconductor package providing PCA methods for incomplete data. Bioinformatics 23, 1164–1167 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2845">75</a></sup>. All SNPs were centered and scaled to unit variance before PCA.</p><p>The genomic makeup of the accessions was analyzed with ADMIXTURE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Alexander, D. H., Novembre, J. &amp; Lange, K. Fast model-based estimation of ancestry in unrelated individuals. Genome Res. 19, 1655–1664 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2852">76</a></sup>. Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig5">5a</a> shows the result for <i>K</i> = 3, which was used to analyze the populations. To resolve admixture along chromosomes, we identified 1283,756 species-specific SNPs in the nonrepetitive regions of 19 chromosomes from fixed differences between the two species as represented by 4 diploid exemplar genomes without evident admixture as described in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">9</a>. These ancestry-informative markers were used to obtain a high-resolution admixture map for the WGS accessions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Fig5">5c</a>), following the method of Wu et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Wu, G. A. et al. Genomics of the origin and evolution of Citrus. Nature 554, 311–316 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d34750e2869">77</a></sup>. A subset of these ancestry-informative markers that overlapped RAD-seq variants were used to infer the segmental ancestry of the RAD-seq accessions. Further details are provided in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">9</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM14">10</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM3">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Data supporting the findings of this work are available within the paper and its Supplementary Information files. A reporting summary for this article is available as a Supplementary Information file. The datasets generated and analyzed during the current study are available from the corresponding author upon request. Genomic reads for the <i>M. sinensis</i> DH1 genome assembly can be found at <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA346689">PRJNA346689</a>, transcriptomic reads at <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA575573">PRJNA575573</a> and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRP017791">SRP017791</a>. The genome, annotation, transcriptomic, and variation data are available on <a href="https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html#!info?alias=Org_Msinensis_er">Phytozome</a>.  <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#Sec17">Source data</a> are provided with this paper.</p>
            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="code-availability" data-title="Code availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="code-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="code-availability">Code availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="code-availability-content">
              
              <p>All custom scripts used for parsing and analyzing transposable elements, gene families, and gene expression, as described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>, are available at GitHub [<a href="https://github.com/miscanthus-paper/Miscanthus-genome.git">https://github.com/miscanthus-paper/Miscanthus-genome.git</a>] and [<a href="https://bitbucket.org/bredeson/artisanal.git">https://bitbucket.org/bredeson/artisanal.git</a>].</p>

                        <span class="c-article-identifiers"><a class="c-article-identifiers__item" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=search&amp;term=Daniel S.+Rokhsar" data-track="click" data-track-action="author link - pubmed" data-track-label="link">PubMed</a><span class="u-hide"> </span><a class="c-article-identifiers__item" href="http://scholar.google.co.uk/scholar?as_q=&amp;num=10&amp;btnG=Search+Scholar&amp;as_epq=&amp;as_oq=&amp;as_eq=&amp;as_occt=any&amp;as_sauthors=%22Daniel S.+Rokhsar%22&amp;as_publication=&amp;as_ylo=&amp;as_yhi=&amp;as_allsubj=all&amp;hl=en" data-track="click" data-track-action="author link - scholar" data-track-label="link">Google Scholar</a></span></p></div></div></li></ol></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="contributions">Contributions</h3><p>D.S.R., K.S., T.M., S.P.M., and M.E.H. provided project leadership. D.S.R., K.S., T.M., S.P.M., A.M.S., B.T.J., G.A.W., and L.V.C. provided figures and wrote the paper. T.M. and J.V.B. assembled the genome and conducted the comparative genomics analysis. N.H.P. generated the HiC assembly. A.M.S. conducted the repeat and allotetraploidy analysis. B.T.J. and M.B.B. conducted the transcriptomic analysis. G.A.W. analyzed the genetic diversity and introgression patterns. S.S. provided the protein-coding gene annotation. H.D. and S.L. provided genetic map data. AB collected samples and provided the transcriptomic, amino acid, and nitrogen data. J.R.H., A.O., and J.E.M. processed samples and extracted nucleic acids for the project. K.F. and I.S.D. contributed the <i>M. sacchariflorus</i> whole-genome sequencing data. J.Gr., J.S., and K.B. coordinated the genome sequencing. K.G. created the double-haploid line and S.J. provided the line. W.B.C. and J.Gi. generated the mapping populations, and J.A.J. and E.J.S. oversaw the generation of the mapping populations. T.Y. provided Ogi63 and Ogi80 triploid lines. J.D.V., L.V.C., S.B., and E.J.S. contributed to RAD-seq data, and J.D.V. and L.V.C. used these data to call variants. M.K., T.H., L.L., X.J., J.P., C.Y.Y., K.H., J.H.Y., and B.K.G. provided miscanthus germplasm.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
                <a id="corresp-c1" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6/email/correspondent/c1/new">Kankshita Swaminathan</a> or <a id="corresp-c2" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-020-18923-6/email/correspondent/c2/new">Daniel S. Rokhsar</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="ethics" data-title="Ethics declarations"><div class="c-article-section" id="ethics-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="ethics">Ethics declarations</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="ethics-content">
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="FPar1">Competing interests</h3>
                <p>Dovetail Genomics LLC is a commercial entity developing genome assembly methods. N.H.P. was an employee of Dovetail Genomics, and D.S.R. is a scientific advisor to and minor investor in Dovetail.</p>
